reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 1D convolution operation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        output_padding (int, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to 0.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, output_padding: int = 0, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 1D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, length).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, length_out).
        """
        return self.conv1d_transpose(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 64
in_channels = 128
out_channels = 128
kernel_size = 3
# much larger signal length for heavier workload
length = 65536

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, length)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

Pseudo-CUDA: ```cpp template <int BLOCK_X, int BLOCK_Y> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int L_in, int K, int stride, int padding, int output_padding, int groups, int L_out) { int batch = blockIdx.z; int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_Y; int o_base = blockIdx.x * BLOCK_X; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int c_out = c_out_base + ty; int o = o_base + tx; // Determine input tile needed int o_start = o_base; int o_end = min(o_base + BLOCK_X, L_out); // compute i_needed range. Use integer math carefully. // For each o in [o_start, o_end-1], i = (o + padding - k) / stride for k in [0, K-1]. // i_min = floor((o_start + padding - (K-1)) / stride) but need floor for negatives. // Let's compute using integer division that floors toward -inf. // In CUDA, for signed ints, division truncates toward zero. So we need a helper. // However, o_start + padding - (K-1) is >= - (K-1) because o_start>=0, padding>=0. // For the first block, it could be negative, e.g., -2. /stride (1) = 0 in truncation, but floor(-2/1) = -2. // So we need floor_div. ``` We need a floor_div for general case. Let's write: ```cpp __device__ inline int floor_div(int a, int b) { // b > 0 if (a >= 0) return a / b; else return - ((-a + b - 1) / b); } ``` Then: ```cpp int i_needed_start = floor_div(o_start + padding - (K - 1), stride); int i_needed_end = floor_div(o_end - 1 + padding, stride); int i_start = max(0, i_needed_start); int i_end = min(L_in - 1, i_needed_end); int tile_len = (i_start <= i_end) ? (i_end - i_start + 1) : 0; ``` Shared memory arrays: ```cpp extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; // size = tile_len * C_in // we can store weight after input if needed ``` We need to know dynamic shared memory size. Let's just use dynamic shared memory, passing size at launch: `tile_len * C_in * sizeof(float) + BLOCK_Y * C_in * K * sizeof(float)`. Load input tile: Each thread loads elements from global to shared. Total elements = tile_len * C_in. Number of threads = BLOCK_X * BLOCK_Y. stride across c_in and i: idx = threadIdx.y*BLOCK_X + threadIdx.x. for (int idx = ty*BLOCK_X + tx; idx < tile_len * C_in; idx += BLOCK_X*BLOCK_Y) { int c = idx % C_in; int i_local = idx / C_in; int i_global = i_start + i_local; if (i_global >= 0 && i_global < L_in) { s_input[i_local * C_in + c] = input[((batch * C_in + c) * L_in) + i_global]; } else { s_input[i_local * C_in + c] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); Load weight tile: float* s_weight = s_input + tile_len * C_in; // shape [BLOCK_Y][C_in][K] or [C_in][BLOCK_Y][K] We need weight layout: (C_in, C_out/groups, K). For groups=1, (C_in, C_out, K). If we store s_weight[ty][c_in][k] = weight[(c_in * C_out + c_out) * K + k]. For each thread t = ty*BLOCK_X + tx: // each thread loads a few weight elements int total_w = BLOCK_Y * C_in * K; for (int idx = t; idx < total_w; idx += BLOCK_X*BLOCK_Y) { int k = idx % K; int tmp = idx / K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; int ty_w = tmp / C_in; int c_out_w = c_out_base + ty_w; if (c_out_w < C_out) { s_weight[((ty_w * C_in) + c_in) * K + k] = weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out_w) * K) + k]; } } __syncthreads(); ``` Then compute: ```cpp float acc = 0.0f; if (c_out < C_out && o < L_out) { if (bias != nullptr) acc = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int numerator = o + padding - k; if (numerator % stride == 0) { int i = numerator / stride; if (i >= i_start && i <= i_end) { int i_local = i - i_start; float inp = s_input[i_local * C_in + c_in]; float w = s_weight[((ty * C_in) + c_in) * K + k]; acc += inp * w; } } } } output[((batch * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + o] = acc; } ``` But we need to handle groups. For groups > 1, C_in and C_out per group. Weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, K). The output channel c_out belongs to group g = c_out / (C_out / groups). The input channels used are only those in the same group: c_in from g*(C_in/groups) to ... So we need to index weight accordingly. Let's adjust for groups: - Let C_out_per_group = C_out / groups. - Let C_in_per_group = C_in / groups. - group id: g = c_out / C_out_per_group. - The weight index: weight[(g*C_in_per_group + c_in_local) * (C_out_per_group) + c_out_local) * K + k] Wait, weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, K). So flatten: weight[(c_in_global * C_out_per_group + c_out_local) * K + k]. Where c_in_global = g*C_in_per_group + c_in_local. And input channel is c_in_global. So in the loop, we can loop over c_in_local from 0 to C_in_per_group-1, and compute c_in_global and weight index. Also, for loading shared input, we need to load all C_in channels because different output channels in the same block may belong to different groups. For each block, we load input tile for all C_in channels and needed spatial range. This is a big contiguous chunk of memory. Each block has to load 128*66 floats = 33.8 KB. At 2 TB/s, loading 33.8 KB per block takes ~17 microseconds. But there are many blocks. Total input memory traffic from global to shared = N * C_out_tiles * spatial_tiles * tile_len * C_in * 4 bytes. Let's compute: N=64, C_out_tiles = 128/4 = 32, spatial_tiles = ceil(65538/64) = 1024. Total blocks = 64*32*1024 = 2,097,152. Each block loads ~66*128*4 = 33,792 bytes. Total input traffic = 2,097,152 * 33,792 = 70.9 GB. This is way more than the input size (2.1 GB). So tiling in shared memory dramatically increases memory read traffic because each block loads its own tile, and input elements are loaded by many blocks (specifically, by all output channel tiles, all batches, and neighboring spatial tiles overlap). In fact, each input element is loaded by all output channel tiles (32) * all batches (64) * number of spatial tiles that cover it (roughly K=3, but with tile overlap of K-1, it's about 3 if tile size small). So total input reads ~ input_size * C_out_tiles * N * 3 = 2.1 GB * 32 * 64 * 3 ≈ 12.9 TB. That's huge! Wait, no: the input is read from global memory into shared memory for each block. Since there are many blocks, total global memory reads are huge. The GPU L2 cache will help: since all blocks in the same spatial tile and same batch but different c_out read the exact same input tile, the L2 cache can serve the data to all 32 C_out blocks after the first reads from memory. Let's analyze. Blocks with same batch and same spatial tile but different c_out tile read the same input data. They run on different SMs or same SM. The first block to read the input tile will bring it to L2. Subsequent blocks reading the same addresses will hit L2, not global memory. L2 bandwidth is much higher (~10 TB/s). But still, reading 33 KB from L2 per block is some overhead. However, the L2 cache size is ~40 MB. How many input tiles fit? 33 KB. Number of input tiles for different c_out tiles and batches and spatial tiles: For a given spatial tile and batch, there are 32 c_out tiles, each reading the same 33 KB. That's 1 MB. L2 can hold many of these. For different spatial tiles, the input tiles are disjoint (with small overlap). For 1024 spatial tiles, total input size is ~2.1 GB, which is larger than L2. So as we sweep through spatial dimension, data will be evicted. But the key reuse is across c_out tiles and batch. For each spatial position, the input data is reused across all output channels and batches. This is a broadcast! In CUDA, if all threads in a warp read the same address, the hardware serves it via a single memory transaction (broadcast). This is very efficient! Wait, is that right? Let's re-evaluate: In the loop over c_in, at iteration c_in, thread computing output channel c_out reads input[n, c_in, i]. All threads in the block (different c_out) read the same input[n, c_in, i] for a given c_in. If these threads are in the same warp, it's a broadcast. If they are in different warps but same block, it's still the same address and will likely hit L1 after the first read. So broadcast is efficient. Now, what about reads across different k? So the memory access pattern is actually very good: - Across c_in: broadcast within warp (same address). - Across o (spatial): coalesced within warp. - Across k: for fixed o, each thread reads up to K contiguous segments offset by 1. Thus a naive thread-per-output-element kernel might have decent memory performance because input reads are either broadcast (c_in loop) or coalesced (spatial loop). This allows the GPU to issue multiple FFMAs in flight without waiting for results. This is a standard optimization. Also, we can ensure the compiler uses FFMA by writing `acc = fmaf(weight, input, acc)`. Because the kernel is compute bound, shared memory is less critical; L2 reuse is enough. For the generic path, we keep the check. Let's write the kernel. Kernel design: - We can tile over output channels and spatial in block. Since threads in a warp have same c_out, for a given k, they read the same weight address (broadcast across c_in? Wait, weight depends on c_in, c_out, k. For a warp, if all threads have same c_out, they read weight sequentially across c_in. That means each thread reads weight[c_in, c_out, k] for c_in=0..127. The addresses are strided by 1536 bytes. No, c_in is the loop variable, same for all threads in the warp at a given iteration. So at iteration c_in=0, all threads in the warp read weight[0, c_out, k] (same address) -> broadcast. At c_in=1, same -> broadcast. So weight accesses are broadcast within warp, which is great. Similarly, input at iteration c_in: all threads in warp read input[n, c_in, i] but i depends on o (spatial). Since o differs across threads, they read consecutive spatial addresses. For fixed c_in and k, i = o - k (or more general). So addresses are consecutive in memory. This is a coalesced load! Excellent. Thus the memory access pattern is: - Broadcast for weight. - Coalesced for input. This is ideal. Now, what about loading input for consecutive k? For k=0, addresses are o+padding (coalesced). For a warp, some threads may have valid i, some may not. If we keep the `if` inside the loop, it causes divergence. For stride=1, this is never needed. Let's do a runtime switch: if stride == 1 && groups == 1, call the fast kernel; else call generic. Even for the fast kernel, we need to handle padding and output_padding? For stride=1, padding can be >0. Output padding adds to L_out. The mapping i = o + padding - k. For k loop, if i in [0, L_in-1], use input[i], else 0. So boundary checks are needed near edges. For L_in=65536 and L_out=65538, the boundary is only 2 elements on each end. But for generic correctness, we need boundary checks. We can structure the kernel to handle edges with `if (i >= 0 && i < L_in)`. Since K is small, this branch is cheap. Thus the fast kernel for stride=1: ```cpp __global__ void conv_transpose1d_stride1_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int L_in, int K, int padding, int L_out, bool has_bias) { int o = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z; if (c_out >= C_out || o >= L_out) return; float acc = has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = o + padding - k; float val = 0.0f; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { val = input[((n * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i]; } float w = weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K) + k]; acc = fmaf(val, w, acc); } } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + o] = acc; } ``` This is very simple. But is it fast enough? Let's think about instruction throughput. Per thread: C_in * K iterations. Each iteration: - Compute i = o + padding - k (can be hoisted partially). - Compute input index. - Branch on bounds. - Load input (global). - Load weight (global). - FFMA. Total instructions: a few integer adds, a compare, loads, FFMA. The compiler will likely unroll the k loop because K is a runtime variable passed to template? Example: ```cpp float acc0 = bias, acc1 = bias; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i0 = o0 + padding - k; int i1 = o1 + padding - k; float v0 = (i0 >= 0 && i0 < L_in) ? input[...] : 0.0f; float v1 = (i1 >= 0 && i1 < L_in) ? input[...] : 0.0f; float w = weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K) + k]; acc0 = fmaf(v0, w, acc0); acc1 = fmaf(v1, w, acc1); } } output[...] = acc0; output[...] = acc1; ``` This doubles the FFMAs per weight load, increasing ILP and reducing relative load overhead. Let me refine: ``` // Block: 256 threads // Handles: batch_b, out_channels [out_c_start, out_c_start+8), output_positions [pos_start, pos_start+32) // Shared memory: input_tile[32 + 2] (for 1 batch, 1 in_channel, 35 consecutive positions) // Actually we might want to process multiple in_channels at once #define LENGTH_TILE 32 #define OUT_C_TILE 8 __shared__ float input_smem[LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1]; // 35 floats // Each thread handles one (out_channel, output_position) int out_c = out_c_start + (threadIdx.x / LENGTH_TILE); // 0-7 int out_pos = pos_start + (threadIdx.x % LENGTH_TILE); // 0-31 float acc = 0.0f; for (int in_c = 0; in_c < in_channels; in_c++) { // Load input into shared memory (only first 35 threads participate) if (threadIdx.x < LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1) { int inp_pos = pos_start + threadIdx.x - (KERNEL_SIZE - 1); if (inp_pos >= 0 && inp_pos < in_length) { input_smem[threadIdx.x] = input[batch, in_c, inp_pos]; } else { input_smem[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float w0 = weight[in_c, out_c, 0]; float w1 = weight[in_c, out_c, 1]; float w2 = weight[in_c, out_c, 2]; // For output position out_pos, need input at out_pos, out_pos-1, out_pos-2 // In shared memory, input at global position p is stored at p - pos_start + (KERNEL_SIZE - 1) int smem_idx = out_pos - pos_start + (KERNEL_SIZE - 1); acc += input_smem[smem_idx] * w0; acc += input_smem[smem_idx - 1] * w1; acc += input_smem[smem_idx - 2] * w2; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, there's an issue. For thread with out_pos = 0, we need smem_idx = 2, 1, 0. For out_pos = 31, smem_idx = 33, 32, 31. Our shared memory has indices 0 to 34. Good. But we need to be careful about precision and performance. The weight indexing weight[in_c, out_c, k] might not be coalesced if threads in a warp are reading different out_c values. Actually, in a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0-31 have out_c = out_c_start + threadIdx.x / 32. But threadIdx.x / 32 for threads 0-31 is 0 (integer division). So all threads in a warp have the SAME out_c! This means 8 warps have out_c from out_c_start to out_c_start+7. Wait, with LENGTH_TILE=32: - threadIdx.x=0-31 → out_c = out_c_start + 0, out_pos = pos_start + 0-31 - threadIdx.x=32-63 → out_c = out_c_start + 1, out_pos = pos_start + 0-31 etc. So in a warp, all 32 threads have the same out_c but different out_pos. When loading weights: - w0 = weight[in_c, out_c, 0] - All threads in a warp need the same w0! Perfect! They can broadcast it from L1 cache. And when accessing input_smem: - Threads in a warp access input_smem[smem_idx] where smem_idx = out_pos + constant - Since out_pos = pos_start + (threadIdx.x % 32), and pos_start is the same for all threads in a warp, threads in a warp access consecutive shared memory locations. - No bank conflicts! (Assuming the offset doesn't cause issues, but for float4... wait, for float, each float is 4 bytes, so consecutive threads access consecutive banks on modern GPUs (32 banks, 4 bytes each), so banks = address/4 % 32. Consecutive threads have threadIdx.x consecutive, so addresses differ by 4 bytes, banks differ by 1. No bank conflicts!) This is actually a nice layout! But we need to be careful about the `__syncthreads()` and warp divergence. The first 35 threads load shared memory, then all threads compute, then syncthreads again. No divergence issue. However, there's one concern: for each in_channel, we do a `__syncthreads()`. For 128 in_channels, that's 128 barriers per block. With 2M blocks, that could be some overhead. Also, we could try to unroll the loop or use tensor cores. Wait, let me think about whether we can use a larger in_channel tile to amortize the syncthreads cost. Suppose we process IN_C_TILE in_channels at a time: - Load input for IN_C_TILE in_channels into shared memory - Process all IN_C_TILE in_channels before next syncthreads For example, IN_C_TILE = 8: - Shared memory: 8 * 35 floats = 280 floats = 1120 bytes - For each out_channel, we loop over 8 in_channels, loading 3 weights per in_channel - Total weight loads per thread: 8 * 3 = 24 floats - This increases register pressure slightly but reduces syncthreads by 8x With IN_C_TILE = 8, we'd have 128/8 = 16 iterations. Each iteration: 1. Load 8 * 35 input floats into shared memory 2. For each of 8 in_channels: load 3 weights, compute 3 FMAs Let me check register usage: - acc: 1 register - w0, w1, w2: 3 registers per in_channel if we load them one at a time - But we can reuse the registers: load w0, w1, w2 for in_channels j, compute, then load for in_channels j+1 - So 4 registers total is plenty! - Block needs 256 threads * ~16 registers = 4096 registers - Per SM limit is 64K registers, so we can have 16 blocks per SM theoretically. - Occupancy would be very high. Actually, I want to check if we can use vectorized loads for weights and inputs. For weight access: weight[in_c, out_c, k] The weight tensor can be transposed beforehand to (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size) for better cache behavior. Actually, weight in PyTorch ConvTranspose1d is stored as (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size). Accessing by [in_c, out_c, k] is coalesced if threads in a warp have consecutive in_c. But in our block, threads in a warp have the same out_c and different out_pos, so they all access the same weight values (which is fine - broadcasts from L1). Actually, since all threads in a warp access the same weight, we might want to preload it once per warp using `__shfl_sync`. But the compiler should optimize this automatically. For input loading into shared memory: ``` if (threadIdx.x < LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1) { input_smem[threadIdx.x] = input[...]; } ``` Only 35 out of 256 threads participate. We could use vectorized loads or have more threads participate. Alternatively, we can have the warp that contains those threads (threads 0-31) do it, or we can just let the compiler handle it. 35 loads is very little. Actually, let me increase the INPUT_TILE. What if LENGTH_TILE = 64? Then we need 66 floats in shared memory. Still tiny. But if LENGTH_TILE = 64, then with 8 out_channels, block size is 512. Let's try different configurations. Option A: LENGTH_TILE=64, OUT_C_TILE=8, block_size=512 - Shared memory: 66 floats - Threads per block: 512 - Blocks per SM (max 2048 threads): 4 blocks - Actually 512 threads * 16 registers = 8192 registers, so 8 blocks per SM possible - Total threads: 2048 - great! - Total blocks: 64 * 128/8 * 65538/64 = 64 * 16 * 1024 = 1,048,576 blocks This might be even better. No: - threadIdx.x=0-63 → out_c_start+0 - threadIdx.x=64-127 → out_c_start+1 - ... - 8 warps per block, each warp handles one out_channel and 64 output positions Wait, warp size is 32. So: - Warp 0: threadIdx.x=0-31, out_c_start+0, out_pos=pos_start+0-31 - Warp 1: threadIdx.x=32-63, out_c_start+0, out_pos=pos_start+32-63 - Warp 2: threadIdx.x=64-95, out_c_start+1, out_pos=pos_start+0-31 etc. Actually, that's fine. Each warp handles one out_channel and 64 output positions (split into two groups of 32 for better instruction pipelining?). Hmm, but warp 0 and warp 1 both handle out_c_start+0. That might cause L1 cache thrashing for weights? No, the weights are broadcast anyway. Actually, having two warps for the same out_channel but different output positions is fine. The weight is cached. Let me think about the total shared memory. If we use IN_C_TILE = 4 and process in tiles: ``` __shared__ float input_smem[IN_C_TILE][LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1]; ``` For IN_C_TILE=4, LENGTH_TILE=64: 4 * 66 * 4 = 1056 bytes. Very small. For IN_C_TILE=16, LENGTH_TILE=64: 16 * 66 * 4 = 4224 bytes. Still small. For IN_C_TILE=32, LENGTH_TILE=64: 32 * 66 * 4 = 8448 bytes. Small. For IN_C_TILE=64, LENGTH_TILE=64: 64 * 66 * 4 = 16896 bytes. Still small. This is all well within the 227 KB limit. But we must also think about how many threads load the shared memory. With IN_C_TILE in_channels and LENGTH_TILE output positions, we need IN_C_TILE * (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1) floats loaded. With IN_C_TILE=32 and LENGTH_TILE=64: 32 * 66 = 2112 floats. Our block has 512 threads, so about 4 floats per thread on average. That's good. Actually, we should think about the memory access pattern for loading input into shared memory. The input tensor is (batch, in_channels, length). If we access input[batch, in_c, pos], with consecutive in_c, these accesses are separated by length elements, so they're NOT coalesced. To coalesce, we want threads in a warp to access either: 1. The same address (broadcast) 2. Consecutive addresses If thread i loads input[batch, in_c_i, pos_i], and in_c_i and pos_i differ, the addresses are batch*in_channels*length + in_c_i*length + pos_i. If in_c_i differs, these are strided by length, not consecutive. So for loading input into shared memory, it's better to have each thread load one (or more) consecutive elements in the length dimension for a single in_channel, and use multiple threads for the same in_channel. Let me redesign the loading: - Map threads to (in_c_idx, pos_idx) - For each in_channel in the tile, 66 threads load 66 consecutive positions - We have 512 threads and need to load 32*66 = 2112 floats - 512 threads can all participate, loading multiple floats per thread Actually, let me use a simpler loading scheme: ``` for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IN_C_TILE * (LENGTH_TILE + K - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int in_c_offset = idx / (LENGTH_TILE + K - 1); int pos_offset = idx % (LENGTH_TILE + K - 1); int in_c = in_c_start + in_c_offset; int pos = pos_start + pos_offset - (K - 1); if (in_c < in_channels && pos >= 0 && pos < in_length) { input_smem[in_c_offset][pos_offset] = input[batch, in_c, pos]; } else { input_smem[in_c_offset][pos_offset] = 0.0f; } } ``` This is coalesced if blockDim.x >= 32 and threads access consecutive idx values. Since idx increases by 1 for consecutive threads (up to wraparound), and the linearization preserves some locality, this should be mostly coalesced. Actually, for the first warp (threads 0-31), idx = 0-31. These map to in_c_offset = 0, pos_offset = 0-31. The addresses are in_channels*length*batch + 0*length + pos. Since pos_offset goes 0-31, the addresses are consecutive! Great coalescing. For the second warp (threads 32-63), idx = 32-63, mapping to in_c_offset = 0, pos_offset = 32-63. Also coalesced. And so on. Good! But what about when we cross in_c boundaries? idx=66 maps to in_c_offset=1, pos_offset=0. The address jumps from batch*in_c*length + 0*length + 65 to batch*in_c*length + 1*length + 0. That's a big jump, but still within L2/DRAM. And since warp access is aligned within itself, each warp is coalesced. One more thing: for very large inputs (64 * 128 * 65536 floats = 2 GB), the memory access pattern is important. The input for a given batch and in_c is contiguous in the length dimension. So as long as threads access consecutive pos, it's coalesced. Now, two important considerations: 1. For output writing: threads in a warp write to consecutive output positions, which is coalesced. 2. For input reading: as designed above, it's coalesced. This should be quite efficient. But I realize there's another optimization: since kernel_size=3 is very small, we might be able to use implicit GEMM or some algorithmic trick. But let's stick with the direct approach for now. Actually, I just realized: for kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, we might be able to implement this using three separate convolutions and summing them. Specifically: output[b, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o] * weight[c_in, c_out, 0] + sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o-1] * weight[c_in, c_out, 1] + sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o-2] * weight[c_in, c_out, 2] Each of these three terms is essentially a matrix multiplication (GEMM): - Term k: input_shifted_by_k[b, c_in, o] * weight_k[c_in, c_out] Where input_shifted_by_k is just input shifted by k positions (with zero padding). So we have three GEMMs and sum them! And each GEMM is batch_size x in_channels x out_channels x output_length... wait. Actually, if we fix the batch dimension, for each batch element, the operation over (out_channels, output_length, in_channels) is a batched GEMM where: - A = input[b, :, :] of shape (in_channels, input_length) - B = weight[:, :, k] of shape (in_channels, out_channels) - C_k = A_shifted_k @ B_k But A_shifted_k is just A with a shift, and B_k is weight[:, :, k]. Wait, but we also have the length dimension. For term k: output_k[b, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o-k] * weight[c_in, c_out, k] If we define X_k[b, c_in, o] = input[b, c_in, o-k] (with zero padding), then: output_k[b, :, :] = X_k[b, :, :].transpose() @ weight[:, :, k] Actually, for a fixed batch and kernel position k: X_k[b, :, :] has shape (in_channels, output_length) where valid entries are shifted. weight[:, :, k] has shape (in_channels, out_channels). So output_k[b, :, :] = X_k[b, :, :]^T @ weight[:, :, k]... wait, dimensions don't match. X_k: (in_channels, output_length) weight_k: (in_channels, out_channels) output_k should be (output_length, out_channels) if we do X_k^T @ weight_k. But our output is (batch, out_channels, output_length). So: output_k[b, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} X_k[b, c_in, o] * weight_k[c_in, c_out] This is: for each o, output[:, o, :] = X_k[:, o, :]^T @ weight_k Or in PyTorch: output_k = torch.matmul(X_k.transpose(1, 2), weight_k).transpose(1, 2) Actually, a simpler view: for fixed b and k: output_k[b] = (weight_k^T @ X_k[b])^T? No. Let's use standard GEMM notation: output_k[b, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} weight_k[c_in, c_out] * X_k[b, c_in, o] For fixed b, this is: output_k[b] = weight_k^T @ X_k[b] where weight_k^T has shape (out_channels, in_channels) and X_k[b] has shape (in_channels, output_length) So output_k[b] has shape (out_channels, output_length). Yes! So we could do: for each of the 3 kernel positions, compute a GEMM. The total would be 3 GEMMs per batch element, or 3 * 64 = 192 GEMMs. But using cuBLAS/cuDNN for 192 GEMMs each of size (128, 128, 65538) might not be efficient due to launch overhead. Alternatively, we could fuse the three GEMMs: output[b] = sum_k weight_k^T @ X_k[b] This is essentially what our custom kernel does directly. Actually, another approach: use the CUTLASS library or similar for optimized GEMMs. But with our custom kernel, we have the advantage that we can fuse the three terms and handle the boundary conditions. Let me think about whether using a GEMM-based approach with some grouping would be better. For all 3 kernel positions combined, for a fixed batch element: output[b] = sum_k weight_k^T @ X_k[b] = [weight_0^T, weight_1^T, weight_2^T] @ [X_0[b]; X_1[b]; X_2[b]] Where the stacked X matrices are interleaved / shifted versions of the input. This isn't a straightforward GEMM because X_0, X_1, X_2 are shifted versions of the same input. However, we can view this as: - Create a strided matrix of shape (3 * in_channels, output_length) where each block of in_channels is a shifted input - Weight matrix of shape (3 * in_channels, out_channels) - GEMM: output = weight^T @ X_extended For in_channels=128, out_channels=128, output_length=65538: - X_extended has shape (384, 65538) - weight_extended has shape (384, 128) - Output has shape (128, 65538) This is a GEMM of (128, 65538, 384) which is M=128, N=65538, K=384. This is a very "tall and skinny" GEMM on the M dimension. Actually, it's more like a wide matrix multiply. Standard GEMM performance for M=128, N=65538, K=384: - Total FLOPs: 128 * 65538 * 384 * 2 ≈ 6.4 GFLOPs per batch element - For 64 batch elements: 410 GFLOPs... wait, that's not right. - Actually: M=128, N=65538, K=384. FLOPs = M * N * K * 2 = 128 * 65538 * 384 * 2 ≈ 6.44 GFLOPs. For 64 batch elements: 412 GFLOPs. - But we said 825 GFLOPs earlier. Why the discrepancy? Ah! I see. In the GEMM formulation, we need to consider that the weight_extended already includes all 3 kernel positions. And X_extended includes the shifted inputs. But we have 64 batch elements. For each batch element, X_extended is different but weight_extended is the same. So it's 64 independent GEMMs: each of size (128, 65538, 384). Total FLOPs = 64 * 128 * 65538 * 384 * 2 ≈ 412 GFLOPs. Wait, but earlier we computed 825 GFLOPs as: 64 * 128 * 65538 * 128 * 3 = 824.6 GFLOPs Where did the factor of 2 go? In matrix multiplication, each output element requires K multiply-adds, which is 2K FLOPs? No, a multiply-add is typically counted as 2 FLOPs in GEMM benchmarks, but in our custom kernel we're just doing 1 FMA = 2 FLOPs. 64 * 128 * 65538 * 128 * 3 = 206 * 2^32 * 3... wait. 64 * 128 * 65538 * 128 * 3 = 64 * 128 * 128 * 3 * 65538 = 3,145,728 * 3 * 65538 Wait: 64 * 128 = 8192. 8192 * 128 = 1,048,576. 1,048,576 * 3 = 3,145,728. 3,145,728 * 65538 = 206,163,853,824 ≈ 206 GFLOPs if we count FMAs as 1 op. If we count FMAs as 2 FLOPs: 412 GFLOPs. Yes! That's where the 2 comes from. So the GEMM formulation is correct: 412 GFLOPs total. Is 412 GFLOPs a lot? For FP32 peak of 67 TFLOPS, that's 412 / 67,000 = 6.1 ms. But for TF32 tensor cores at 989 TFLOPS, it's 0.42 ms. However, the GEMM dimensions (128, 65538, 384) are quite non-square. The M=128 dimension is very small. cuBLAS might not handle this very efficiently for FP32, but with TF32 tensor cores it could be very fast. But wait, we need fp32 precision. Using TF32 would reduce precision to 1e-3 or so, which might violate the 1e-4 tolerance. So we probably need to stick with FP32. For FP32 GEMM of (128, 65538, 384): - This is essentially a batched GEMV operation. 128 x 384 times 384 x 65538. - Peak FP32 is 67 TFLOPS, but achieving this on small GEMMs is hard. Actually, we could use the CUTLASS library for optimized small GEMMs. But PyTorch already uses cuDNN which likely calls into cuBLAS with some optimizations. Hmm, but our custom kernel approach might be competitive or better because: 1. We avoid the overhead of creating the extended input matrix (which would require memory allocation and data movement). 2. We can fuse the boundary condition handling. 3. For small M (128), a custom kernel with good tiling might outperform cuBLAS if cuBLAS has significant fixed overhead. Let me now actually look at what PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d does under the hood. It likely calls into cuDNN, which uses various algorithms including GEMM-based, FFT-based, or Winograd-based approaches. For kernel_size=3, cuDNN might use Winograd, which is very efficient. Let me think about another optimization: using float4 for vectorized memory access. Since our output positions are contiguous, we can have each thread compute 4 output positions and use float4 for input/weight loads where possible. Actually, a powerful optimization would be to have each thread compute multiple output positions and use multiple accumulators to increase ILP. For example, each thread computes 4 consecutive output positions with 4 separate accumulators: ``` float acc0 = 0, acc1 = 0, acc2 = 0, acc3 = 0; for (int in_c = 0; in_c < in_channels; in_c++) { float w0 = weight[in_c, out_c, 0]; float w1 = weight[in_c, out_c, 1]; float w2 = weight[in_c, out_c, 2]; float i0 = input[batch, in_c, base_pos + 0]; float i1 = input[batch, in_c, base_pos + 1]; float i2 = input[batch, in_c, base_pos + 2]; float i3 = input[batch, in_c, base_pos + 3]; acc0 += i0 * w0 + i_1 * w1 + i_2 * w2; acc1 += i1 * w0 + i0 * w1 + i_1 * w2; ... } ``` Wait, for output position o, we need input at o, o-1, o-2. For output positions o, o+1, o+2, o+3: - o needs input[o], input[o-1], input[o-2] - o+1 needs input[o+1], input[o], input[o-1] - o+2 needs input[o+2], input[o+1], input[o] - o+3 needs input[o+3], input[o+2], input[o+1] So the inputs needed are: input[o-2], input[o-1], input[o], input[o+1], input[o+2], input[o+3]. That's 6 unique values. For 4 output positions, each needs 3 inputs, so 12 total input accesses but only 6 unique values. But wait, each thread needs: - i0 = input[o-2], i1 = input[o-1], i2 = input[o], i3 = input[o+1], i4 = input[o+2], i5 = input[o+3] Then: acc0 = acc0 + i2*w0 + i1*w1 + i0*w2 acc1 = acc1 + i3*w0 + i2*w1 + i1*w2 acc2 = acc2 + i4*w0 + i3*w1 + i2*w2 acc3 = acc3 + i5*w0 + i4*w1 + i3*w2 This is 6 loads + 4*3 = 12 FMAs per in_channel. The FMAs are somewhat interdependent but consecutive FMAs use different accumulators. Actually, this might not be the best for ILP because the FMAs for acc0 need i2, i1, i0 which come from the same registers. But the FMAs themselves are mostly independent accross the 4 accumulators. With 12 FMAs per in_channel and 128 in_channels, that's 1536 FMAs per thread. With 4-way ILP (4 accumulators), the latency might be reasonably hidden. But we also need to consider memory bandwidth. If each thread loads 6 input floats per in_channel, and there are 256 threads, that's 1536 loads per in_channel per block. For 128 in_channels, that's 196,608 loads per block. At 4 bytes each, that's 786 KB per block. With L1 cache capacity of ~100 KB per SM, we can't cache all this. Actually, the same input is used by many blocks! Input for a given (batch, in_c) is 65536 floats = 256 KB. This doesn't fit in L1 but does fit in L2 (50 MB total). For 64 batch elements and 128 in_channels, total input is 2 GB. L2 cache is 50 MB, so it can hold about 2.5% of the input. With 2M blocks accessing different parts of input, L2 hit rate might be decent for some patterns but not great for others. This is getting complex. Let me try a simpler approach first: implement the basic tiled kernel and see how it performs. Then optimize based on profiling. Let me draft a kernel with these parameters: - Block size: 256 threads - Grid: (batch_blocks, out_channel_blocks, length_blocks) - Each block handles: 1 batch, 8 out_channels, 32 output positions... OR 1 batch, 1 out_channel, 256 output positions Let me try both and see. Actually, let me try a completely different approach that's often very fast for convolutions with small kernels: the Winograd algorithm. For kernel_size=3, Winograd F(2,3) or F(4,3) is very popular. Winograd converts the convolution into element-wise multiplications in the transformed domain. For F(2,3): - Process input in tiles of 2 output positions - For each tile, transform input (length 4) and kernel (length 3) - Element-wise multiply in transform domain - Inverse transform to get 2 output positions This reduces the number of multiplications from 2*3=6 to 4 (for F(2,3)), a 1.5x speedup. For F(4,3): - Tile size 4 output positions - Transforms require 6 multiplications instead of 4*3=12, a 2x speedup. Winograd is typically used in cuDNN for small kernels. If cuDNN already uses Winograd, can we beat it? Custom Winograd might be hard to implement and optimize correctly. And a simple well-tiled direct convolution might already be close. Let me think about another approach: reinterpret the operation as a matrix-vector multiplication across channels and use warp-level matrix multiply (WMMA) or similar. Actually, for in_channels=128 and out_channels=128, that's relatively large! We have 128 input channels and 128 output channels. This is large enough that a GEMM-based approach should work well. Wait, I just had an idea. Since kernel_size=3 is small, and the shifts are just by 0, 1, 2, we could literally implement this as three separate pointwise convs (matrix multiplications) and sum them. For kernel position k: output_k[b, c_out, o] = sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o-k] * weight[c_in, c_out, k] This is a 1x1 convolution on a shifted input! Actually no, it's just a matrix multiplication for each output position. But across positions, it's a batched matrix multiply. We can implement each k as: 1. Shift input by k positions (with zero padding) 2. Do a 1x1 convolution (which is just matrix multiply across channels) A 1x1 convolution for (64, 128, 65538) input and (128, 128) weights would be a GEMM. PyTorch's 1x1 conv uses highly optimized cuDNN/cuBLAS implementations. But doing three 1x1 convs and summing them involves: 1. Three separate kernel launches 2. Or two with accumulation 3. Plus the shifting Actually, we can avoid shifting by just using sliced views: - For k=0: use input[..., 0:length] - For k=1: use input[..., 0:length-1] shifted, with a zero at the end - For k=2: use input[..., 0:length-2] shifted, with two zeros But doing this with matrix multiplies would still require creating intermediate tensors. Alternatively, we can implement this as a single matrix multiply by expanding the input appropriately. But that expansion itself is a memory copy. What if we use a custom kernel that's basically a GEMM wrapper but handles the shifting implicitly? That is, instead of tiling over output positions, we tile over channels and do GEMM-like accumulation. Actually, I think the most practical approach is the directly tiled kernel I described earlier. Let me implement it and test. Key design decisions: 1. Block size: 256 threads 2. Block assignment: 1 batch, 8 out_channels, 32 output positions = 256 threads 3. Shared memory: Input tile for IN_C_TILE in_channels and LENGTH_TILE+2 positions 4. Process in_channels in tiles of IN_C_TILE Wait, let me reconsider the shared memory design. If a block handles multiple out_channels (8), then all threads in the block need the same input data (for a given in_channel). So loading input to shared memory and sharing it across the 8 out_channels is very efficient. Let me try: - IN_C_TILE = 16 - OUT_C_TILE = 8 - LENGTH_TILE = 32 - Block size = 8 * 32 = 256 threads Shared memory: input_smem[16][34] = 544 floats = 2,176 bytes For each block: 1. But each warp also handles 32 output positions, so 1 thread per output position. Yes! That's perfect. Each thread in a warp handles exactly 1 output position. And a warp handles 32 contiguous output positions for 1 out_channel. For loading input to shared memory: - 544 floats to load - 256 threads available - Each thread loads 2 or 3 floats (with some thread idling) For the computation: ``` for in_c_tile = 0 to in_channels/IN_C_TILE: load input_smem[IN_C_TILE][34] __syncthreads() for in_c_offset = 0 to IN_C_TILE: float3 w = load_weight(in_c_tile*IN_C_TILE + in_c_offset, out_c); // Each thread computes for its output_pos int smem_base = threadIdx.x % 32; // 0-31 for output positions // Actually threadIdx.x % 32 gives the pos_offset within the tile int pos_offset = threadIdx.x % 32; // For output position corresponding to pos_offset, need input at: // global_pos = block_pos_start + pos_offset // input at global_pos - k for k=0,1,2 // In smem: input_smem[in_c_offset][pos_offset + 2 - k]? Now, there's a potential issue with threadIdx.x % 32. In CUDA, the modulo operation can be expensive. But since 32 is a power of 2, `threadIdx.x & 31` is used, which is free. Also, `threadIdx.x / 32` for warp ID can be computed as `threadIdx.x >> 5`. And out_c = block_out_c_start + warp_id. For loading weights: `weight[in_c, out_c, k]`. All threads in a warp access the same 3 weight values. This is a broadcast from global memory, which is very efficient thanks to L1 cache. For loading shared memory: threads in a warp access `input_smem[in_c_offset][pos_offset + constant]`. Since pos_offset is `threadIdx.x & 31`, consecutive threads access consecutive shared memory locations. No bank conflicts. For storing output: threads in a warp write to consecutive output positions. Coalesced. This looks really good! Let me now think about register usage. Each thread needs: - acc: 1 float - w0, w1, w2: 3 floats (or loaded sequentially to use fewer registers) - loop variables, indices: a few integers - Total: ~8-12 floats + a few ints With 255 registers max per thread, and 64K registers per SM, the occupancy bottleneck is not registers. It's likely the number of thread blocks per SM. With 256 threads per block and max 2048 threads per SM, we can have at most 8 blocks per SM. With shared memory of only ~2KB per block, and 227KB limit, that's not a bottleneck either. So we can achieve 100% occupancy (8 blocks * 256 threads = 2048 threads per SM). One optimization: instead of loading weights 3 times per in_channel (one for each k), we can load them once and reuse. Actually, our code already does this. Warp 1 requests weight[in_c, out_c_start+1, 0:3] (12 bytes). These 12-byte requests for consecutive out_c values might be combined by the L1 cache into a single 64-byte or 128-byte transaction if they fall in the same cache line. 128 bytes = 32 floats. 12 bytes = 3 floats. 32/3 ≈ 10.7. So about 10-11 warps' worth of weights fit in one cache line. This is good! The L1 cache can serve multiple warps' weight requests with a single transaction. Now, for the custom kernel code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define KERNEL_SIZE 3 #define OUT_C_TILE 8 #define LENGTH_TILE 32 #define IN_C_TILE 8 __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length ) { int batch = blockIdx.x; int out_c_start = blockIdx.y * OUT_C_TILE; int length_start = blockIdx.z * LENGTH_TILE; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // 0-7 int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // 0-31 int out_c = out_c_start + warp_id; int out_pos = length_start + lane_id; if (batch >= batch_size || out_c >= out_channels || out_pos >= out_length) { return; // Actually, we need to handle boundary properly } // But we need all threads to participate in shared memory loading // So we should not return early. } ``` Wait, we can't return early because all threads need to load shared memory. Since `active` depends on `out_pos` which differs per lane, we have divergent execution within warps. Is there a way to avoid this? For the last block, we could just have fewer threads, but CUDA blocks have fixed sizes. Alternatively, we can let all threads compute and only mask the output write: ``` float acc = 0.0f; // ... compute for all threads ... if (active) { output[...] = acc; } ``` This avoids divergence during computation. The extra computation for out-of-bounds lanes is very small (1 block out of 2049). So it's fine. Wait, but what about reading out-of-bounds weights? Actually, let me think about how to handle weight bounds safely without hurting performance for the common case. For out_c >= out_channels, we can just not read weights and not write output: ``` if (out_c >= out_channels) { // Don't participate in computation // But still participate in __syncthreads() } ``` In CUDA, we can't use `return` if there are `__syncthreads()` in the loop. Actually, if out_c is the same for all threads in a warp, then `valid_out_c` is the same for all threads in a warp! So there's no divergence for weight access within a warp. Each warp is either fully valid or fully invalid for out_c. And for out_pos, all threads compute regardless, but only write if valid. To maximize compute throughput on H100: 1. Keep occupancy high (many warps per SM to hide latency) 2. Maximize ILP within each thread (multiple independent FMAs) 3. Minimize dependencies between instructions 4. Avoid stalls from shared memory bank conflicts or barrier synchronization With our design, we have 8 warps per block and up to 8 blocks per SM = 64 warps per SM. On H100, each SM can hold many warps, and 64 warps provides good latency hiding. For ILP: currently each thread does 3 FMAs per in_channel, all dependent (they accumulate to `acc`). We can increase ILP by having each thread compute multiple output positions. For example, each thread computes 2 output positions: - pos_offset = lane_id * 2 and lane_id * 2 + 1 - But lane_id only goes 0-31. For computation, each thread computes 2 accumulators: ``` float acc0 = 0.0f, acc1 = 0.0f; for (int in_c_off = 0; in_c_off < 8; in_c_off++) { float w0 = weight[...]; float w1 = weight[...]; float w2 = weight[...]; int base_pos = lane_id * 2; acc0 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 2] * w0; acc0 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 1] * w1; acc0 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 0] * w2; acc1 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 3] * w0; acc1 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 2] * w1; acc1 += input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 1] * w2; } ``` Interleaving the FMAs for acc0 and acc1 would help ILP: ``` float acc0 = 0.0f, acc1 = 0.0f; for (int in_c_off = 0; in_c_off < 8; in_c_off++) { float w0 = weight[...]; float w1 = weight[...]; float w2 = weight[...]; int base_pos = lane_id * 2; float i0 = input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 0]; float i1 = input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 1]; float i2 = input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 2]; float i3 = input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + 3]; acc0 += i2 * w0; acc1 += i3 * w0; acc0 += i1 * w1; acc1 += i2 * w1; acc0 += i0 * w2; acc1 += i1 * w2; } ``` Now we have alternating FMAs on acc0 and acc1, which are independent. On modern GPUs, this significantly improves ILP and throughput. Latency of FMA is ~4 cycles, so with 2 independent accumulators, we can hide about half the latency. We need FP32. One more idea: use CUTLASS or cuBLAS for the GEMM formulation. But the GEMM dimensions are special (M=128, N=65538, K=384 for one batch element). Actually, with 64 batch elements, we can batch them: M=128, N=65538, K=384, batch=64. cuBLAS GEMM for these dimensions: batch=64, M=128, N=65538, K=384. Each GEMM is 128 x 65538 x 384. The M dimension is very small. For standard cuBLAS, batched GEMM with small M might not be very efficient. However, if we transpose the problem: Instead of computing output = weight^T @ X for each batch, we could view the whole thing as a larger GEMM. Actually, let me think about it. If we stack all batch elements: A = weight_extended = (3*128, 128) = (384, 128) For each batch element b, X_b is (384, 65538) where the rows are shifted input. But X_b is not a simple slice - it's constructed from shifted input. We'd need to create this matrix explicitly. Actually, if we think of the operation as: For each output position o: output[:, :, o] = sum_k input[:, :, o-k] @ weight_k^T Where input[:, :, o-k] has shape (batch, in_channels) and weight_k has shape (in_channels, out_channels). So for each o, it's a matrix multiply of (batch, in_channels) @ (in_channels, out_channels) = (batch, out_channels). For 65538 output positions, that's 65538 GEMMs of (64, 128) @ (128, 128). Each GEMM: M=64, N=128, K=128. Total FLOPs: 65538 * 64 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 137.4 GFLOPs (with FMA as 2 ops). Wait, earlier I said 412 GFLOPs. For each (b, out_c, o): 384 multiplies and 383 adds ≈ 384 FMAs. Total: 64 * 128 * 65538 * 384 = 206 GFLOPs (FMA as 1 op). Or 412 GFLOPs (FMA as 2 ops). For the GEMM formulation per output position: output[:, :, o] = sum_k input[:, :, o-k] @ weight_k^T For each k: (64, 128) @ (128, 128) = (64, 128). FLOPs per k: 64 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 2,097,152. For 3 k values: 6,291,456 FLOPs per output position. For 65538 positions: 6,291,456 * 65538 = 412.3 GFLOPs. Matches! Now, if we do 65538 GEMMs of (64, 128, 128), each is very small but there are many of them. Can we batch them? With batch=65538, M=64, N=128, K=128. This could actually work well in cuBLAS if we use strided batched GEMM. But wait, for each k, the input[:, :, o-k] depends on o. So we can't easily batch all positions into one GEMM for a fixed k because the inputs are different slices. Actually, we CAN! In PyTorch, if we reshape input from (batch, in_channels, length) to (batch * length, in_channels), then: output = input_reshaped @ weight_0^T would give (batch * length, out_channels). But we need output_0 which has shape (batch, out_channels, length). Actually, for k=0: output_0[b, out_c, o] = sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o] * weight_0[c_in, out_c] This is exactly: output_0 = input.transpose(1,2) @ weight_0 where input.transpose(1,2) has shape (batch, length, in_channels) and weight_0 has shape (in_channels, out_channels). In batched form, for each batch element: input_b.transpose(0,1) @ weight_0, where input_b.transpose(0,1) is (length, in_channels). So it's a batch of 64 GEMMs: (65536, 128) @ (128, 128) = (65536, 128) for each batch element. M=65536, N=128, K=128. FLOPs per batch: 65536 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 2.15 GFLOPs. Total for 64 batches: 137.4 GFLOPs... wait. For k=0: one GEMM per batch element. batch_size=64, M=65536, N=128, K=128. Wait, output_length for k=0 term is not 65536. For term k, we shift input by k, so valid o ranges from k to L_in-1+k. Actually, for k=0: output_0[b, out_c, o] = sum_{c_in} input[b, c_in, o] * weight_0[c_in, out_c] This is valid for o = 0 to L_in-1 = 65535. For o=65536, input[b, c_in, 65536] is out of bounds, so output_0 is 0. Similarly for k=1: valid for o=1 to 65536. For k=2: valid for o=2 to 65537. To get the final output: output[b, out_c, o] = output_0[b, out_c, o] + output_1[b, out_c, o] + output_2[b, out_c, o] Where each output_k has different valid ranges and out-of-range values are 0. So we could do: 1. GEMM for k=0: input[:, :, 0:65536] @ weight_0^T → temp0 of shape (64, 128, 65536) 2. GEMM for k=2: input[:, :, 0:65536] @ weight_2^T → temp2 of shape (64, 128, 65536), then pad with 2 zeros at the beginning 4. Sum the padded results: output = [0, temp0] + [0, temp1] + [0, temp2] with appropriate alignment Actually, more precisely: - temp0[b, out_c, o] for o=0..65535 - temp1[b, out_c, o] for o=0..65535 should be placed at output positions o+1 - temp2[b, out_c, o] for o=0..65535 should be placed at output positions o+2 So: output[b, out_c, 0] = temp0[b, out_c, 0] output[b, out_c, 1] = temp0[b, out_c, 1] + temp1[b, out_c, 0] output[b, out_c, 2] = temp0[b, out_c, 2] + temp1[b, out_c, 1] + temp2[b, out_c, 0] ... output[b, out_c, 65537] = temp2[b, out_c, 65535] This involves: - 3 GEMMs of (64, 65536, 128, 128) - Element-wise sum with shifted alignment The GEMMs are large! M=65536, N=128, K=128 for each batch. In total, 64 * 65536 = 4,194,304 rows, 128 cols, 128 inner dim. This is a batched GEMM where all batches have the same dimensions and the batch size is 64. M = 65536, N = 128, K = 128. This is very non-square! M is huge, N and K are tiny. cuBLAS can handle this. With strided batched GEMM: - A strided: each batch is (65536, 128) with stride 65536*128 - B strided: each batch is (128, 128) with stride 0 (same weights for all batches!) - C strided: each batch is (65536, 128) with stride 65536*128 Actually, B is the same for all batches, so we can just not use batching and do a single GEMM if we reshape properly. If we reshape A from (64, 65536, 128) to (64*65536, 128) = (4194304, 128), and B is (128, 128), then: C = A @ B^T gives (4194304, 128). Then reshape C to (64, 65536, 128). This is a single GEMM: M=4,194,304, N=128, K=128. M is very large, N and K are small. This is a "tall and skinny" GEMM. cuBLAS might handle this reasonably well, especially with Tensor Cores (TF32). But again, TF32 precision might be an issue for 1e-4 tolerance. For FP32, tall and skinny GEMMs typically achieve 50-70% of peak. At 67 TFLOPS peak, maybe 30-40 TFLOPS effective. Total FLOPs: 4194304 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 137.4 GFLOPs per k value. For 3 k values: 412 GFLOPs. Time at 40 TFLOPS: 412 / 40,000 = 10.3 ms. Now, our custom kernel: Total FLOPs: 206 GFMAs = 412 GFLOPs (same). If we achieve 80% of peak FP32: 0.8 * 67 = 53.6 TFLOPS. Time: 412 / 53,600 = 7.7 ms. The GEMM approach through cuBLAS is 3 large GEMMs plus shifts/sums. The shifts and sums are extra memory operations. And creating temp tensors adds memory traffic. So the custom kernel might definitely be faster. Let me finalize: I'll write a custom tiled kernel. One more idea: use `__ldg` (load global through read-only cache) for weights. For loading input from global memory to shared memory, __ldg might help but shared memory is write-through. The explicit loads with bounds checks are fine. OK, let me write the kernel now. Actually, one more optimization: partial loop unrolling and warp-level primitives for reduction? No, we're not doing reduction across threads. For the weight memory layout: PyTorch stores ConvTranspose1d weights as (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size). So weight[in_c, out_c, k] has address: weight + in_c * out_channels * kernel_size + out_c * kernel_size + k To get better cache behavior, I could transpose weights to (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size) at initialization time. Then weight[out_c, in_c, k] has address: weight_t + out_c * in_channels * kernel_size + in_c * kernel_size + k In the kernel, for a fixed out_c and varying in_c, consecutive in_c values would be consecutive in memory. This might improve cache behavior during the in_c loop. Let me do this! In `ModelNew.__init__`, after copying the weights, I'll transpose them: ```python self.weight = self.weight.permute(1, 0, 2).contiguous() ``` Then in the kernel, weight access is: ``` float w0 = weight_t[out_c * in_channels * KERNEL_SIZE + in_c * KERNEL_SIZE + 0]; ``` For consecutive in_c values, addresses differ by KERNEL_SIZE = 3 floats = 12 bytes. This is within a cache line (32 floats = 128 bytes), so consecutive in_c loads will be in the same or adjacent cache lines. Excellent! And since we're loading weights sequentially in the inner loop, this is great for cache locality. Actually wait, each thread in a warp loads weights for the SAME out_c but DIFFERENT in_c values (since they iterate through in_c together). But all threads in a warp iterate through in_c together and load the SAME in_c at the SAME time. So in_c is the same across the warp. The only difference is out_c, which is constant per warp. With the transposed layout, for a fixed out_c and in_c, the 3 weight values are consecutive. For the next in_c, the next 3 values are consecutive. So the access pattern is sequential within a cache line, which is fantastic for prefetching. Let me verify the weight shape: - Original: (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size) - Transposed: (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size) For our test: (128, 128, 3). In the kernel, with transposed weights: ``` int weight_base = out_c * in_channels * KERNEL_SIZE + in_c * KERNEL_SIZE; float w0 = weight_t[weight_base + 0]; float w1 = weight_t[weight_base + 1]; float w2 = weight_t[weight_base + 2]; ``` For the next in_c_off (in_c + 1): ``` int weight_base_next = out_c * in_channels * KERNEL_SIZE + (in_c + 1) * KERNEL_SIZE; ``` Difference = 3 floats. Consecutive in memory. With `in_c` going from 0 to 127 for a fixed out_c, we read 128 * 3 = 384 consecutive floats. This is 1536 bytes, which fits in a few cache lines (128 bytes each, so ~12 cache lines). Actually, that's 12 cache lines, which might not all fit in L1. But with prefetching, this should still be fast. If multiple blocks for the same out_c run on the same SM, the weights stay in L2 cache. This transposed format is a good optimization. Let me include it. Now for the CUDA code. I'll write a complete module. One potential issue: when using `load_inline`, I need to make sure the C++ wrapper function correctly handles tensor shapes and launches the kernel. Template: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define KERNEL_SIZE 3 #define OUT_C_TILE 8 #define LENGTH_TILE 64 #define IN_C_TILE 8 #define LENGTH_PER_THREAD 2 __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length ) { // Implementation } ''' cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight_t, int out_length ) { // Launch kernel } ''' conv_transpose1d = load_inline( name='conv_transpose1d', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['conv_transpose1d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], ) ``` Wait, `--use_fast_math` might introduce precision issues. Actually, `--use_fast_math` can make `__fdividef` and some math functions less accurate, but we're not using any transcendental functions, just FMAs. So `--use_fast_math` should be fine and might enable some optimizations. However, `--use_fast_math` can also change the rounding behavior slightly. For a sum of products, it won't matter much. Let me write the complete kernel now. Key details: - Block size: 256 threads (8 warps) - Block assignment: - x: batch index - y: out_channel block (0, 8, 16, ..., 120) - z: output length block (0, 64, 128, ...) - Each block processes: 1 batch, 8 out_channels, 64 output positions - Each warp processes: 1 out_channel, 64 output positions (32 threads * 2 per thread) - Each thread processes: 2 output positions for 1 out_channel Shared memory layout: ``` __shared__ float input_smem[IN_C_TILE][LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1]; // IN_C_TILE=8, LENGTH_TILE=64, so 8*66=528 floats ``` Thread mapping: ``` int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // 0-7 int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // 0-31 int out_c = blockIdx.y * OUT_C_TILE + warp_id; int base_pos = lane_id * LENGTH_PER_THREAD; // 0, 2, 4, ..., 62 int length_start = blockIdx.z * LENGTH_TILE; ``` Wait, `base_pos` is the offset within the block's tile. The actual output positions are `length_start + base_pos` and `length_start + base_pos + 1`. For loading shared memory: ``` for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IN_C_TILE * (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int in_c_off = idx / (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); int pos_off = idx % (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); int in_c = in_c_base + in_c_off; int pos = length_start + pos_off - (KERNEL_SIZE - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (in_c < in_channels && pos >= 0 && pos < in_length) { val = input[batch * in_channels * in_length + in_c * in_length + pos]; } input_smem[in_c_off][pos_off] = val; } ``` For the compute loop: ``` float acc0 = 0.0f, acc1 = 0.0f; for (int in_c_base = 0; in_c_base < in_channels; in_c_base += IN_C_TILE) { __syncthreads(); // Load input into shared memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; ...) { ... } __syncthreads(); // Compute for (int in_c_off = 0; in_c_off < IN_C_TILE; in_c_off++) { int in_c = in_c_base + in_c_off; if (in_c >= in_channels) break; int weight_base = out_c * in_channels * KERNEL_SIZE + in_c * KERNEL_SIZE; float w0 = weight_t[weight_base + 0]; float w1 = weight_t[weight_base + 1]; float w2 = weight_t[weight_base + 2]; int smem_base = base_pos; // 0, 2, 4, ..., 62 float i0 = input_smem[in_c_off][smem_base + 0]; float i1 = input_smem[in_c_off][smem_base + 1]; float i2 = input_smem[in_c_off][smem_base + 2]; float i3 = input_smem[in_c_off][smem_base + 3]; acc0 += i2 * w0; acc1 += i3 * w0; acc0 += i1 * w1; acc1 += i2 * w1; acc0 += i0 * w2; acc1 += i1 * w2; } } ``` Wait, I need to check the shared memory indexing carefully. For output position `length_start + base_pos + j` (j=0 or 1): We need input at `length_start + base_pos + j - k` for k=0,1,2. In shared memory, global position `p` maps to smem_idx = p - length_start + (KERNEL_SIZE - 1). So input at `length_start + base_pos + j - k` maps to smem_idx = base_pos + j - k + 2. For j=0: - k=0: smem_idx = base_pos + 2 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 2] = i2 - k=1: smem_idx = base_pos + 1 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 1] = i1 - k=2: smem_idx = base_pos + 0 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 0] = i0 So acc0 = i2*w0 + i1*w1 + i0*w2. ✓ For j=1: - k=0: smem_idx = base_pos + 3 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 3] = i3 - k=1: smem_idx = base_pos + 2 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 2] = i2 - k=2: smem_idx = base_pos + 1 → input_smem[...][base_pos + 1] = i1 So acc1 = i3*w0 + i2*w1 + i1*w2. ✓ Great, the indexing is correct. For output writing: ``` int out_pos0 = length_start + base_pos; int out_pos1 = length_start + base_pos + 1; if (out_c < out_channels) { if (out_pos0 < out_length) { output[batch * out_channels * out_length + out_c * out_length + out_pos0] = acc0; } if (out_pos1 < out_length) { output[batch * out_channels * out_length + out_c * out_length + out_pos1] = acc1; } } ``` For the shared memory access pattern: threads in a warp access `input_smem[in_c_off][base_pos + offset]`. `base_pos = lane_id * 2`. So consecutive lane_ids have base_pos = 0, 2, 4, 6, ... They access `input_smem[in_c_off][lane_id*2+constant]`. Since lanes access even positions with stride 2, bank conflicts? Shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes for float. Address = base + in_c_off * smem_stride + (lane_id * 2 + offset) * sizeof(float). Banks accessed = (lane_id * 2 + offset) % 32. For lane_id=0: banks 0 + offset For lane_id=1: banks 2 + offset For lane_id=2: banks 4 + offset ... For lane_id=15: banks 30 + offset For lane_id=16: banks 32 + offset = 0 + offset (mod 32) For lane_id=17: banks 34 + offset = 2 + offset (mod 32) So lane 0 and lane 16 both access bank offset! And lane 1 and lane 17 both access bank 2+offset! This is a 2-way bank conflict for every access! Oh no! Bank conflicts will halve the effective shared memory bandwidth. This is bad. How to fix? We need consecutive threads to access consecutive banks. Option 1: Use float2 for shared memory and have each thread handle 2 consecutive output positions that map to consecutive banks. But we already have each thread handling 2 positions. If `base_pos = lane_id * 2`, the starting bank is `lane_id*2 % 32`. Lanes 0 and 16 collide. Option 2: Change the thread-to-output mapping. Instead of `base_pos = lane_id * 2`, we could have warp handle 1 out_channel and all 64 positions, but thread mapping should ensure consecutive threads access consecutive output positions. Wait, consecutive threads in a warp already access consecutive lane_ids. The issue is that the shared memory indices have stride 2. What if we transpose the shared memory? Instead of `[in_c_off][pos_off]`, use `[pos_off][in_c_off]`? No, that doesn't help because the bank conflicts come from different threads accessing different pos_offs. Actually, the bank is determined by the address. For `input_smem[in_c_off][pos_off]`: address = &input_smem[0][0] + in_c_off * (LENGTH_TILE+2) + pos_off bank = (address / 4) % 32 For fixed in_c_off: bank = (in_c_off * (LENGTH_TILE+2) / 4 + pos_off / 4) % 32? Wait, no. Each float is 4 bytes, so bank is determined by the byte offset divided by 4, mod 32. bank = (in_c_off * (LENGTH_TILE+2) + pos_off) % 32. For in_c_off=0, pos_off goes from 0 to 65. Banks = pos_off % 32. So for pos_off=0: bank 0, pos_off=1: bank 1, ..., pos_off=31: bank 31, pos_off=32: bank 0. If threads access pos_off = lane_id * 2, lane_id * 2 + 1, lane_id * 2 + 2, lane_id * 2 + 3: For lane_id=0: pos_off=0,1,2,3 → banks 0,1,2,3 For lane_id=1: pos_off=2,3,4,5 → banks 2,3,4,5 ... For lane_id=15: pos_off=30,31,32,33 → banks 30,31,0,1 For lane_id=16: pos_off=32,33,34,35 → banks 0,1,2,3 Bank conflicts! Lanes 0 and 16 both access banks 0 and 1 (for different pos_off values). Actually, each thread accesses 4 pos_offs. For lane 0, the 4 accesses are to banks 0,1,2,3. For lane 16, the 4 accesses are to banks 0,1,2,3. The memory controller can only serve one thread per bank per cycle. So if lanes 0 and 16 both try to access bank 0 in the same instruction, one has to wait. This is a 2-way conflict. How to completely avoid bank conflicts? Option A: Pad the shared memory. If we add 1 float of padding after each row, then banks shift. `input_smem[in_c_off][pos_off]` where the second dimension has size `LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1 + 1`. Bank = (in_c_off * 67 + pos_off) % 32. For in_c_off=0: bank = pos_off % 32. Same issue. Actually, padding the second dimension by 1 might not help if threads still access pos_off = lane_id * 2. Option B: Reorder threads. Have thread mapping such that consecutive threads access consecutive pos_offs. If each thread handles 2 positions, and we want threads 0-31 to access pos_offs 0-63 consecutively: - Thread 0 handles pos 0 and 1 (base_pos=0) - Thread 1 handles pos 2 and 3 (base_pos=2) ... This is what we have, but it causes conflicts. Wait, let me think about this differently. The bank conflict happens when two threads in a warp access the same bank. With pos_off = lane_id * 2: Lane 0 accesses pos_off ∈ {0, 1, 2, 3} → banks {0, 1, 2, 3} Lane 16 accesses pos_off ∈ {32, 33, 34, 35} → banks {0, 1, 2, 3} All 4 banks conflict between lanes 0 and 16. Similarly, lanes 1 and 17 conflict on banks {2,3,4,5}, etc. If we instead used `base_pos = lane_id` (1 position per thread), there would be no conflicts since consecutive lanes access consecutive banks. So using LENGTH_PER_THREAD=1 avoids bank conflicts (for reading from shared memory)! For LENGTH_PER_THREAD=2, we have conflicts. Can we avoid them? If we change the layout or access pattern: Instead of each thread computing 2 consecutive positions, what if each thread computes 2 interleaved positions? For example, thread i computes positions i and i+32. Then base_pos for position 1 is i, base_pos for position 2 is i+32. Lane 0: pos 0, 32 → banks 0, 0 (since 32%32=0). Conflict within the same thread! Actually, the same thread accessing the same bank in consecutive instructions is fine because it's the same thread. The memory controller can serve the same thread sequentially. Lane 0: banks 0, 0 for pos 0 and 32 Lane 1: banks 1, 1 for pos 1 and 33 ... No conflicts between lanes! This is great! But wait, if a thread accesses pos 0 and pos 32 for the same in_c_off, and both map to bank 0, but for different rows in shared memory: Actually, bank = (in_c_off * 66 + pos_off) % 32. For in_c_off=0: pos=0: bank 0 pos=32: bank 0 But positions 0 and 32 are 32 floats apart, which is exactly the bank cycling period. They both map to bank 0. For consecutive accesses by the same thread, the hardware might pipeline them. No issue. For different lanes: Lane 0: pos_off=0 → bank 0, pos_off=32 → bank 0 Lane 1: pos_off=1 → bank 1, pos_off=33 → bank 1 ... Lane 15: pos_off=15 → bank 15, pos_off=47 → bank 15 Lane 16: pos_off=16 → bank 16, pos_off=48 → bank 16 ... No conflicts between different lanes! Because each lane accesses unique banks. However, within a single instruction, a thread only accesses one pos_off at a time. So for the first FMA (using pos_off for the first accumulator), there's no conflict. For the second FMA (using pos_off+32 for the second accumulator), there's also no conflict. But wait, if we use `base_pos = lane_id` and `second_pos = lane_id + 32`, then: For the first position (j=0): smem_idx = lane_id + 2 - k For the second position (j=1): smem_idx = lane_id + 32 + 2 - k For k=0: Lane 0: smem[2] and smem[34] Lane 1: smem[3] and smem[35] ... Lane 15: smem[17] and smem[49] Lane 16: smem[18] and smem[50] ... All banks unique within each instruction! This is perfect. No bank conflicts! So the access pattern for the two accumulators uses: i2 = smem[lane_id + 2] and i34 = smem[lane_id + 34] Wait, for the second position (lane_id + 32): smem_idx = (lane_id + 32) + 2 - k = lane_id + 34 - k For k=0: lane_id + 34 For k=1: lane_id + 33 For k=2: lane_id + 32 All these are within the shared memory size (66) for lane_id <= 31: lane_id + 34 max = 65. Valid. But pos_off = lane_id + 32 needs to be within the loaded range. We load pos_off from 0 to 65. lane_id + 32 goes from 32 to 63. Valid. And the output positions for the second accumulator are `length_start + lane_id + 32`. With LENGTH_TILE=64, the block covers positions length_start to length_start+63. Lane i covers position i (first accumulator) and i+32 (second accumulator). This works! But wait, there are only 32 threads in a warp. If each warp handles 1 out_channel, and each thread handles 2 positions (i and i+32), then the warp handles 64 positions total. But there are only 32 threads per warp. So block size = OUT_C_TILE * 32 = 8 * 32 = 256 threads. Each block handles 8 out_channels and 64 positions. This matches our design! And there are no bank conflicts. The only issue: the two output positions computed by a thread are separated by 32. They are not consecutive. For writing output: Thread i writes to positions `length_start + i` and `length_start + i + 32`. Let me write the complete ModelNew class. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define KERNEL_SIZE 3 #define OUT_C_TILE 8 #define LENGTH_TILE 32 #define IN_C_TILE 8 __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight_t, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_length, int out_length ) { int batch = blockIdx.x; int out_c_start = blockIdx.y * OUT_C_TILE; int length_start = blockIdx.z * LENGTH_TILE; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // 0-7 int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // 0-31 int out_c = out_c_start + warp_id; int out_pos = length_start + lane_id; __shared__ float input_smem[IN_C_TILE][LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1]; float acc = 0.0f; for (int in_c_base = 0; in_c_base < in_channels; in_c_base += IN_C_TILE) { // Load shared memory for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IN_C_TILE * (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); idx += blockDim.x) { int in_c_off = idx / (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); int pos_off = idx % (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); int in_c = in_c_base + in_c_off; int pos = length_start + pos_off - (KERNEL_SIZE - 1); float val = 0.0f; if (in_c < in_channels && pos >= 0 && pos < in_length) { val = input[batch * in_channels * in_length + in_c * in_length + pos]; } input_smem[in_c_off][pos_off] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (out_c < out_channels) { #pragma unroll for (int in_c_off = 0; in_c_off < IN_C_TILE; in_c_off++) { int in_c = in_c_base + in_c_off; if (in_c >= in_channels) break; int weight_idx = out_c * in_channels * KERNEL_SIZE + in_c * KERNEL_SIZE; float w0 = weight_t[weight_idx + 0]; float w1 = weight_t[weight_idx + 1]; float w2 = weight_t[weight_idx + 2]; int smem_idx = lane_id + (KERNEL_SIZE - 1); acc += input_smem[in_c_off][smem_idx] * w0; acc += input_smem[in_c_off][smem_idx - 1] * w1; acc += input_smem[in_c_off][smem_idx - 2] * w2; } } __syncthreads(); } if (out_c < out_channels && out_pos < out_length) { output[batch * out_channels * out_length + out_c * out_length + out_pos] = acc; } } ''' cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight_t, int out_length ) { int batch_size = input.size(0); int in_channels = input.size(1); int in_length = input.size(2); int out_channels = weight_t.size(0); auto output = torch::zeros({batch_size, out_channels, out_length}, input.options()); dim3 grid(batch_size, (out_channels + 7) / 8, (out_length + 31) / 32); dim3 block(256); conv_transpose1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight_t.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, in_channels, out_channels, in_length, out_length ); return output; } ''' # Compile conv_transpose1d = load_inline( name='conv_transpose1d', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['conv_transpose1d_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '-arch=sm_90'], ) ``` Wait, I notice a potential issue: the grid launch uses `(out_channels + 7) / 8` as a direct integer division in C++. New configuration: - LENGTH_TILE = 64 - OUT_C_TILE = 4 - IN_C_TILE = 8 - Block = 128 (4 warps) - Grid: batch=64, out_c_tiles=32, length_tiles=1025 Wait, another idea: what if each thread computes output for multiple output channels? Not just multiple length positions. Let me reconsider. A warp has 32 threads. If we want each thread to compute 2 length positions, we need 64 length positions per warp. We could also have each thread compute 4 length positions (LENGTH_TILE=128). But then shared memory is IN_C_TILE * (128 + 2) * 4 = 8 * 130 * 4 = 4160 bytes. Still fine. But block size would be 128 for 4 warps, each handling 128 positions = 128/32 = 4 positions per thread. Wait, each warp handles 1 output channel, and each thread in the warp handles 4 positions: lane_id, lane_id+32, lane_id+64, lane_id+96. That's a lot of registers (4 accumulators). Let me try with 2 positions per thread and IN_C_TILE=16. Actually, let's just try to maximize work per thread: - Block = 128 (4 warps) - OUT_C_TILE = 4 (1 output channel per warp) - LENGTH_TILE = 128 (4 positions per thread: lane_id, +32, +64, +96) With IN_C_TILE=16: - SMEM = 16 * 130 * 4 = 8320 bytes This is reasonable. Let me code this up. The idea is each thread does more FMAs before writing, which is good for memory-bound kernels. Wait, but the problem is we need to verify it's still correct. The indexing with lane_id+32, +64, +96 still maps correctly as long as each output position uses the right shared memory index. Let me write this carefully: For output position `out_pos = length_start + lane_id + 32*i`: - `smem_idx = lane_id + 32*i + (KERNEL_SIZE - 1)` - `acc[i] += smem[smem_idx] * w0` - `acc[i] += smem[smem_idx - 1] * w1` - `acc[i] += smem[smem_idx - 2] * w2` With LENGTH_TILE=128, shared memory index ranges from 0 to 129 (128 + 3 - 1 = 130). For lane_id=31, i=3: smem_idx = 31 + 96 + 2 = 129. Perfect, fits. Actually, lane_id max is 31, i=3 → 31 + 96 + 2 = 129. And shared memory is 130 elements. Good. Now for the last tile, some positions might be >= out_length. We handle that. With this configuration: - Grid: batch=64, out_c_tiles=32 (128/4), length_tiles=512 (65538/128 ≈ 512) - Total blocks: 64 * 32 * 512 = 1,048,576 blocks - Threads per block: 128 - Total thread invocations: 134M (vs 134M for the original, same total work) Wait, 64 * 128 * 65538 total output elements. Each thread computes 4 elements. Total threads = 64*128*65538/4 = 134M. With 128 threads per block, blocks = 134M/128 = 1.05M. Yes, correct. This reduces the number of blocks by 2x compared to the original, which might reduce launch overhead and caching issues. Let me try this version. Actually, since we're only doing FMAs, fast math doesn't apply. Let me compile and test correctness for this more optimized version. Actually, I realize one issue: with `blockDim.x = 128` and `threadIdx.x >> 5`, we get warp_id in [0, 3], which is 4 warps. OUT_C_TILE should be 4. For loading shared memory: `for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < IN_C_TILE * (LENGTH_TILE + KERNEL_SIZE - 1); idx += blockDim.x)` With IN_C_TILE=16, pad=2 → 16*130=2080 elements.
